Mound (or Tav&#351;anl&#305; H&ouml;y&uuml;k) in Turkey's K&uuml;tahya province&mdash;located in the west of the Anatolian peninsula.The mound is referred to as the \"Heart of Western Anatolia\" because it appears to resemble a heart shape from above the ground.Recent excavations at the site, led by researchers at Bilecik &#350;eyh Edebali University, resulted in the discovery of the rare brain and skin remains.The remnants belong to a young man aged 15-18 at the time of death, and another middle-aged man who was 40-45 years old when he died, the Anadolu Agency (AA) reported.Researchers believe these individuals died when their homes were set on fire and they were unable to flee during an attack around 3,700 years ago.According to archaeologist Erkan Fidan at Bilecik &#350;eyh Edebali University, who led the research, the mound is the oldest settlement in the area, and likely served as the capital of the region in the Bronze Age.Evidence suggests there was a large-scale attack on the city around 1700 B.C. that resulted in the entire settlement being burned to the ground.The preservation of brain material inside the skull was the result of the fact that both skeletons were seemingly exposed to high levels of heat, Y&#305;lmaz Selim Erdal from Hacettepe University's Anthropology Department told AA.The archaeologists also found skin remnants in one of the skeletons between the chest and the abdomen, which had also been carbonized by heat.Carbonization is the process of material being converted into carbon, typically by heating or burning, or during fossilization.The latest findings represent the first time that such skin remains have been found during archaeological excavations in the country. The preserved remains of brain material, on the other hand, have been found four or five times previously, the researchers said.The archaeologists are now investigating what the purpose of the attack on the settlement was, and who may have been responsible.The latest findings were presented at a conference organized by the European Association of Archaeologists that was held in Belfast, Northern Ireland, between August 30 and September 2 this year.Do you have a tip on a science story that Newsweek should be covering? <picture class="mapping-embed" width="1200" height="800"><source type="image/webp" media="(min-width: 992px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.webp?w=790&amp;f=e7d2dc3a9c6380697e4a3cb6bb605fa0 1x"><source type="image/jpeg" media="(min-width: 992px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.jpg?w=790&amp;f=e7d2dc3a9c6380697e4a3cb6bb605fa0 1x"><source type="image/webp" media="(min-width: 768px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.webp?w=900&amp;f=0392e9d78b2bedfec03d53cfe81e746f 1x"><source type="image/jpeg" media="(min-width: 768px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.jpg?w=900&amp;f=0392e9d78b2bedfec03d53cfe81e746f 1x"><source type="image/webp" media="(min-width: 481px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.webp?w=790&amp;f=e7d2dc3a9c6380697e4a3cb6bb605fa0 1x"><source type="image/jpeg" media="(min-width: 481px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.jpg?w=790&amp;f=e7d2dc3a9c6380697e4a3cb6bb605fa0 1x"><source type="image/webp" media="(min-width: 0px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.webp?w=450&amp;f=3042da70d4ea46c23bc04bec6d65bf81 1x"><source type="image/jpeg" media="(min-width: 0px)" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.jpg?w=450&amp;f=3042da70d4ea46c23bc04bec6d65bf81 1x"><source type="image/webp" srcset="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.webp?w=1200&amp;f=e72341d92c9e1753cd3825e897d19c66"><img loading="lazy" class="mapping-embed imgPhoto" id="i2279025"  src="https://d.newsweek.com/en/full/2279025/set-human-remains.jpg?w=1200&amp;f=e72341d92c9e1753cd3825e897d19c66" alt="A set of human remains" width="1200" height="800" /></picture></div><figcaption class="caption ar23-caption">
<span class="cap" id="short-cap-description">Stock image: An archaeologist excavating a set of human remains. Researchers have discovered the well-preserved brain and skin remnants of people who lived around 3,700 years ago. </span>

<span class="credit ar23-credit">iStock</span>
</figcaption> <script>function toggleCap(sourceEvent){const siblings=sourceEvent.parentNode.children;const shortDesc=siblings["short-cap-description"];const fullDesc=siblings["full-cap-description"];const readMoreBtn=sourceEvent;if(fullDesc.style.display==='none'){shortDesc.style.display='none';fullDesc.style.display='inline';readMoreBtn.textContent='Less'}else{shortDesc.style.display='inline';fullDesc.style.display='none';readMoreBtn.textContent='More'}}</script> </figure><p>The remnants belong to a young man aged 15-18 at the time of death, and another middle-aged man who was 40-45 years old when he died, the Anadolu Agency (AA) reported.</p><p>Researchers believe these individuals died when their homes were set on fire and they were unable to flee during an attack around 3,700 years ago.</p><p>According to archaeologist Erkan Fidan at Bilecik &#350;eyh Edebali University, who led the research, the mound is the oldest settlement in the area, and likely served as the capital of the region in the Bronze Age.</p><p>Evidence suggests there was a large-scale attack on the city around 1700 B.C. that resulted in the entire settlement being burned to the ground.</p>	<figure class="block block-ibtg-article fA" data-gtm-category="Related In-Text A" data-gtm-action="Click"><div class="read-content read-more-embedded"><div class="v_embed" id="v_embed"><div class="style2 id"><div class="block-title">Read more</div><ul class="read-ul"><li class="read-arrow-icon">

<a data-gtm-category="Related In-Text A" data-gtm-action="Click" data-gtm-label="Article 3" href="/cemetery-50-bodies-unearthed-medieval-monastery-england-1824454">  Cemetery with 50 bodies, other rare finds, unearthed at medieval monastery</a></li></ul></div></div></div>
</figure><p>The preservation of brain material inside the skull was the result of the fact that both skeletons were seemingly exposed to high levels of heat, Y&#305;lmaz Selim Erdal from Hacettepe University's Anthropology Department told AA.</p><p>The archaeologists also found skin remnants in one of the skeletons between the chest and the abdomen, which had also been carbonized by heat.</p><p>Carbonization is the process of material being converted into carbon, typically by heating or burning, or during fossilization.</p><p>The latest findings represent the first time that such skin remains have been found during <a href="https://www.newsweek.com/skeletons-worlds-oldest-city-disease-violence" target="_blank" rel="noopener" class="multivariate">archaeological excavations</a> in the country. The preserved remains of brain material, on the other hand, have been found four or five times previously, the researchers said.</p><p><a href="https://www.newsweek.com/rare-ancient-roman-swords-1900-years-ago-found-desert-cave-1824953" target="_blank" rel="noopener" class="multivariate">The archaeologists</a> are now investigating what the purpose of the attack on the settlement was, and who may have been responsible.</p><p><a href="https://www.newsweek.com/cemetery-50-bodies-unearthed-medieval-monastery-england-1824454" target="_blank" rel="noopener" class="multivariate">The latest findings</a> were presented at a conference organized by the European Association of Archaeologists that was held in Belfast, Northern Ireland, between August 30 and September 2 this year.</p><p><strong>Do you have a tip on a science story that <em>Newsweek</em> should be covering? Do you have a question about archaeology?
